RATING: 7
Based on one play.  Multiple routes to victory and these seem to be available to be worked out before you even start.  Pick which one you want, buy a building and away you go.  You just have to hope that no-one else buys your follow-up building(s), though they probably won't want to because it would be sub-optimal for them.
It was a good game but it seems to me that the game is just a comparison of how those pre-planned paths to victory interact with each other.

RATING: 8.5
Role selection and resource management, with a hand managment aspect that makes for a very lively game.  There seem to be a lot of routes to victory, with shipping and building being the most valuable (sort of like another game I can think of).  It can be very interactive with several players, as being a turn to late for shipping or building can be disastrous.  The voting for the laws at the end of each round can be tense.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A Victory Point (VP) grabbing game that on the surface seems derivative, but actually plays quite differently or at least feels relatively fresh.

The game plays over 6 rounds, and each player has an identical set of 5 character cards, 4 of which will be played as actions during each round.  Each character has a unique ability(s), as well as a voting strength.  Turn order can be important but not always crucial, and it changes depending on the highest character card (voting power) played last in the round.

The actions consist of such things as acquiring resources from your individual player boards, building buildings onto (and OVER) your resources on your player board (some get you VPs directly, and all buildings are worth 2VP at the end), buying/selling goods and products at the market, activating buildings special abilities on your player boards, and loading ships with goods/products for VPs.  Sounds similar to roles in Puerto Rico, but these are done individually, they are not shared actions.

At the beginning of each round, proposed bills are viewed, and at the end of the actions, these are voted upon using your last character card and money (blind-bid).  The highest vote total selects 2 bills to enact, and these are primarily for scoring points because you can meet the condition(s) and/or screw other players over.  Repeat this for 6 rounds, and the highest score wins.

Now it might not sound original, but after a couple of plays, it's been enjoyable and again, feels reasonably different enough from other games I own.  It also scales great from 2-5 players.  Luck is minimal in that the bills are semi-random each round, but you can control which ones actually come into play, and the most recent ship each round is random, but there is building to change that, and of course you don't even need to ship that much to win anyways.  In the couple of games I've played, I've won by focusing mainly on money and voting, and then I've also won by focusing on shipping and building.  There seems to be several viable ways to score points and win.

Own the [boardgame=40794]El Presidente[/boardgame] expansion, and the [boardgame=64461]Canadian Embassy[/boardgame], the [boardgame=60683]Sambaschule[/boardgame], and the [boardgame=36521]Spielecafe[/boardgame] promo tiles.

RATING: 4
No depth of game play, you make your strategy choice on turn one as the buildings available are fixed and lock you in to a particular strategy, after this choice is made, the ships that come out that have the available goods spaces are random, the big kicker is the legislation changes each turn, these drastically change the game and often dictate which strategy will win, if you don’t have control you will probably lose, if controlling it spends all of your resources, you will probably lose, if the legislation cards you need for your strategy come out on the last turn, you will probably lose. 

It’s a gambling game as to which strategy will win, but you won’t know which strategy is best until the last turn and you lock yourself in turn 1. I can’t see what there is to like about a game with ever closing options and opportunities.

RATING: 7
A very strong worker placement game - one of my favorites! Better than Puerto Rico, since it is more intuitive and all the things you need to know are clearly laid out in the board game area. A very visually attractive game as well! The only downside with the game setup is organizing the buildings - ugh, there could have been better game design around this.

The El Presidente expansion sounds interesting, but since I'm now trying not to buy into expansion hype, I won't be getting this unless we start playing the base game a lot (not likely). 

Update: After some musings, I realize that Cuba is so great because it takes the good stuff in worker placement games and puts it into a pretty, streamlined package. 2015 UPDATE: Well, this one is out as we haven't played it in a long time and my husband likes Stone Age better.

Tracked on family game list (grooven).

RATING: 8
Un juego de mesa de lo mas divertido, una curiosa mezcla entre pilares de la tierra, Caylus y Puerto Rico.

Personalmente me encanta, se me hacia algo corto para desarollar segun que estrategias, pero con la expansion de El Presidente se soluciona. El juego con la expansión para mi es sobresaliente, sin ella solo notable.

Me parece mas divertido Cuba que Puerto Rico, con mas opciones de juego y que escala mucho mejor con dos jugadores.

RATING: 7
A fun and worthy game with some interesting mechanics. I enjoyed the law selection mechanic, the card order of play considerations, and the endgame penultimate and final round planning.

Somewhat to my surprise, I did not find planning to pay for taxes and duties to get victory points be to onerous or unpleasant in this game (my thoughts turn toward Year of the Dragon, Walnut Grove and Trajan where "necessity mechanics" can feel like a pain). 

Cuba is not necessarily fireworks, but it is an engaging and well designed game. On the next play, I will probably enjoy planning more, in particular, which buildings to buy, and where to advantageously place them on my plantation. Interesting game.

RATING: 6.5
Of the Essen 2007 releases this is getting the most table time, which is incongruous as it is by no means my favourite.  It has similarities to Pillars, Puerto Rico and Caylus.  However it appears not to deliver in many aspects: (i) the buildings do not appear worth acquiring - in my most recent game my strategy of buying no buildings won me the game, (ii) the rum and cigars are not worth producing and (iii) the game induces in me a level of indecisiveness through the multitude of choices (which is a little different to AP) that I have not encountered in any other game.

On the plus side, the game shines as a two player strategy game where there is plenty to plan and no hidden information.  Despite my many misgivings about the game I am happy to explore and play it further (as a 2 or 3 player game).  Maybe there is a smaller streamlined game there waiting to break through!  As it stands I think aspects of this game don't work as they were intended but, as mentioned, I am happy to devote the time to prove this to myself!

RATING: N/A
Just read the rules. I think the core rules sound very interesting. There should be minimal downtime. The bills/acts makes sure that no game is a like. Great looking board and components. I am not a fan of blind bids at all but I think it fits here very well with buying votes for the bills to pass. I think it is very thematic. My only complaint is the set number of rounds to play the game. I tend to like games that has a variable end condition, which adds another level of depth. The game has very minimal luck but there are so many variables, the game won't feel dry.  Also, the theme is shining through very well.  This is really really looking good. I am looking forward to it.  

I need to re-read the Amyitis rules and see how they compare to each other. That will affect if I should put Cuba in "Buy Now" list.  

After further review, I think I am going to have to pass on this game.  Even though blind bid mechanic fits the theme very well, I don't think I'll get over the frustration I derive from this mechanic.  Also, the random ships makes the game very tactical, since you cannot really plan ahead for them.  I guess you could do a house rule of placing all the ships in order to be used so that people can plan for them.  Hmm... seems like an easy fix.  Blind bidding is my biggest hang up at the moment.

Just read a wonderful review and unfortunately, this goes in the "Try before Buy" pile.

It took me a while to get this on the table, partly because I thought it may be too complicated for a lot of folks.  There are a good number of rules, but it flows better than I thought it would.  In our first game, 3 of the 4 of us kind of stumbled through... especially me.  After explaining the rules, I (as start player) used the tradesewoman to buy a tobacco for 4 pesos that I could have taken with an alternative action and I had a tobacco spoil at the end of the round.  That's about as dumb as it gets, but I still enjoyed the game.  I undervalued the parliamentary phase of the game.  I look forward to trying this again to see if it's gonna stick around or just be kind of clever and go away.

Second game played was also very good and with the same group.  Seems like we all had a better idea what we were doing.  I ended up with a lot of money and other than bidding 8 pesos for nothing one round, I felt like the money was helpful.  I really like this game so far... I'm not sure how many times it could be played and still loved,  but I think it has a lot of potential.

First two plays were very long with a lot of social interaction.  I played it once with more focus from everyone at the table and liked it again.  Seems like a tough game to get to the table, but it's good when it gets there.

RATING: 6
Cuba is a mix of just about every early to mid-2000's euro game.  Worker placement, VP transformations, resource management, role selection, etc.  It's a beautiful board, the components are nice.... and I was entirely underwhelmed by it.  While it does pretty much everything, it does not do anything particularly well. My overall impression is that I'm entirely underwhelmed by the game and I really don't see why I'd play it over other games in my collection that while not doing everything, do something better.  Perhaps it's just that this game has been eclipsed by better games while not really measuring up and earning the classic status of it's contemporary counterparts Caylus and Puerto Rico.

RATING: 5
First play was a bit underwelming, I felt I'd seen this sort of game before, certainly seen the majority of the mechanisms and it felt like mulitplayer solo and your ability to plan wrecked by the randomness of the laws. 2nd play, opinion was revised, knowing what laws are in the piles really makes a difference, now you can plan for when a law comes into play - saving the right resources and putting yourself in a position to benefit, game is all about optimising your actions - and where to place the buildings. 3rd play, now I'm not playing this multiplayer solo anymore, I'm looking at the other players and trying to predict what they are going to do .... for me this game needs multiple playings for the systems to click - it seems to scale from 2-5 quite well, shipping is easier with less of course. Is it a solid keeper? I'm not sure, but I expect to get 20 or so playings out of it before it gets stale.

**** Update, played it again and bored with it now, expansion is insanely expensive ... traded it away and I don't miss it.

RATING: 7.8
The sound of the fan whirring overhead and the light scent of cigar smoke wafting into the cafe, compete with your friends in managing Cuban plantations.

Awesome game-Love it!! There are 3-4 solid scoring paths or engines you get churning and to shoot for in this game.  In turn one you begin to sort out potential paths and then in rounds 2-3 you are honing in on your real strategy.  Lots of scoring ops give you options along the way to round out your effort, and it reminds one of Puerto Rico. My advice is: sit where you can see the buildings and their various descriptions left available for purchase. (similar to Puerto Rico)

The Parlimentary vote mechanic is pretty nifty allowing for you to subvert other players successes and I have yet to see anyone really dominate within that aspect.

My one complaint of this lovely game is that I wish it went maybe even one more cycle though as the game ends a little soon or abruptly.  It seems like right around when you feel your scoring is really going get firing on all 8, the game ends abruptly.

RATING: N/A
A number of glowing reviews caused me to math trade for Cuba.  I read the rules multiple times, and then set it up for a 3er solo game to make sure I understood it.  By the time I had the game set up, I was ready to trade it.  The main issue is the buildings - there are 25, and they all do different things, and they are all available to purchase from the start.  This is a ridiculously large decision space for those I game with, and is what made me decide to trade the game away.  With the right player aid - and the right players - this might not be an issue, but it was for me. 

The rest of the game has a similar level of detail.  Each decision made has significant long term effects that must be considered.  One starts with 5 cards which represent actions.  The order that one takes these actions matters, and the action that is not selected also matters.  How one moves their manager on their personal board matters not just for this turn, but probably for the next one as well.  

Overall, the game looks like it all comes together really well.  But the level of detail and long range planning involved were prohibitive for those I play with.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
Fue un gran descubrimiento este juego. Es un juego de selección. Cada jugador dispone de 5 personajes y una finca donde hace sus construcciones y recolecta sus recursos. Durante un turno (se juegan seis), cada jugador va a utilizar cuatro personajes. El quinto va a servir para votar leyes (apoyado por dinero en subasta oculta).
El trabajador va a recolectar productos y recursos de tu finca, según su ubicación (recoger todo lo que se encuentre en la fila x y columna y, si su ubicación es x,y). Además, en la finca se pueden construir edificios que otorgan ciertas habilidades. Para ello hay que usar otro personaje (el capataz), que "activa" los edificios, también según la ubicación del trabajador (o uno cualquiera). Esto hace que el manejo del tablero de cada jugador de que pensar.. :).
Además, existe la posibilidad de acudir al mercado, con precios variables, para comprar/vender productos y vienes (vendedora). También se puede "exportar" bienes y productos, para convertirlos en puntos de victoria (con el alcalde) y usar el arquitecto para construir edificios en tu finca (pagando coste en recursos y normalmente habilitan para ciertas transformaciones).

Además, y como guinda, en cada turno se aprueban dos leyes que modifican alguna regla del juego. LAs leyes están divididas en cuatro equipos. Las dos primeras permiten comprar directamente puntos de victoria a difernetes precios en materiales/dinero. La tercera otorga puntos según la característica de tu finca y la cuarta impone ciertas limitaciones y hace algunas reglas más estrictas.

En definitiva, un juego en el que hay mucho que pensar durante la partida y se hace muy emocionante. Altamente recomendable.

Vendido en la bsk a fermanowar en octubre 2014 por 25€

RATING: 7
This is a beautiful brain-burner of a resource conversion game, with plenty of conflict and traumatic decision-making throughout. If it suffers from anything, it's that all the choices are offered from the very start; the turns don't have a script, against which you can easily base a strategy for your game. For example, in Puerto Rico, you're collecting cash to begin with, and shipping your goods or filling up your city from the mid-game; and the buildings scale up in cost so you get access to them as your game develops. By contrast, in Cuba, you can probably buy most of the buildings on Turn 1, because you don't really get a production engine going, it's running well enough from the start.

RATING: 5
Cuba is one of those games that takes many different mechanics and synthesizes them into a singular experience.  Some may call this "borrowing," others "ripping off," but I don't really care what you call it, as long as it works.  And Cuba works - it just isn't that much fun.

For 4 or 5, I could see this being ideal, brutal and chaotic.  For two, there is little tension.  In fact, the 2er is a little too unbalanced, as the shipping route becomes too valuable to ignore, leading to a shipping-directed game.  This is easily fixable (just eliminate the 3VP ship), but even fixed, the game has too little tension to be interesting.  Getting anywhere in the game takes a while, as the VP machine is a long one to build.  If the game were a couple of turns longer, it might be long enough to generate some interest by building up a VP engine, but as it is, the game ends a little early.  I know, the expansion fixes this - but is $30 worth it just to improve a mediocre experience into a possibly decent one?  I doubt it.

Cuba is not bad, and some will like it.  As a 2er, though, it just kind of falls flat - you end up wanting to play one of the games it is imitating, which likely does its one novel mechanism amazingly, rather than this, which has many bland-ish options.  Should be the ideal Euro, but for us, it just didn't quite get there.

RATING: 5
Second impressions were more favorable. I actually think Cuba is a fairly clever resource management/kingdom building game, but it is absolutely killed by the slow pace and the downtime, of which there is a lot. A lot of analysis and look-ahead has to be done to accomplish much of anything, but there is extremely little interactivity to the game, and certain bottlenecks make it hard to plan too far ahead. I think Cuba works as a game and has plenty of interesting things happening, but I think it just takes too long to play with too much downtime to be worth it.

It's also a fixed-fun game where more players is better. I think the game really wants 5, but the pacing was bad enough with 4.

A game I would like to like, and if it came in at two-thirds of the length I'm pretty sure I would, but as it is I think it's going direct to the trade/sale pile.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
[13/11{2007] The designers of this workman-like and thoroughly unoriginal design meshed together various mechanisms found in Puerto Rice, Caylus and Säulen der Erde. I will concede it works, but is it fun?!? Its action selection mechanism isn't nearly as interesting as Puerto Rico's role selection or even Caylus's workers. I put this down to the level of player interaction being (yet) lower than in those two games. Really, it seems far more of a game in which you implement a once-conceived plan rather than one in which interaction with other players may dramatically alter your course during a game.

In Cuba, players focus on perfecting their own little economic puzzles. Many "heavy" Euros have such a multiplayer solitaire character, so there must be a sizable gamer demographic into such accountant-style gaming. I myself am bored stiff by this sort of brooding play, particularly when playing time is as long as in the case of Cuba.

The solitaire puzzle aspect of one's personal board is remiscent of Princes of Florence's Tetris-style building aspect. Here you need to work out your best layout of your industries on a number of rows and columns. Just a variation of the tetris-style layouting.... Yawn.

How come the game was called Cuba?!? Its pace is so pedestrian and there's no Caribbean flair whatsoever! One would hope that the laws and voting might create excitement, but it's nothing more than a not particularly interesting closed-fist-auction. Compare that to the voting in say In the Shadow of the Emperor. There you have to devine what way others will vote. The whole psychological aspect is woefully underdeveloped. Talk of a missed opportunity!

Tediously long and overwrought. Little player interaction. Multiplayer solitaire... Cuba is everything I don't want a Euro to be.

RATING: 3
I so so WANTED to like this, I really did, so why did I give it only a 3?
Is it broken? No, so why won't I play it again?
Because I think the VP model is slightly wonky;
i) processed goods - taking an extra action to 'prepare' - get just as many VP as raw goods
ii) the margin of victory is so low, that whoever goes first, and builds the  2VP building stands a VERY good chance of winning by 12 VP - the very margin that that building gives them. In every game I have played, I have win with that building. In all of the last few, when others knew how to play, by exactly 12 VP!

I have other gripes, but basically, I've sold my copy and won't be buying another

RATING: 6
This is very much a build your own victory points engine in isolation from the other players. It is less like the Caylusy Puetro Rico it had been described to me as. Each turn you select four out of five actions you have as cards: produce resources, ship resources, buy/sell resources, activate buildings or build a building. It is not like Puerto Rico though because the selection only really affects you and you don't block someone using the action or activate a phase for all players. The only similarity to Caylus is that you buy buildings but noone else can use them. It is a pretty game and plays quickly but it just doesn't stand out from a sea of cube manipulators.

RATING: 6

Although clearly derivative, the mechanics blend together in unique ways to create an engaging game.  Nice that it scales to two players, although haven't played enough with that number to know if it's better or worse than with other numbers.

Economic with buying and selling at the market, getting resources and products you can change for refined goods or victory points...having enough money and resources  to do everything, very tight game and could be prone to AP in some folks. Excellent game and we're thinking of getting the expansion.

RATING: 4
It took me a while to realise what it is that Cuba attempts to accomplish. It looks like a standard resource snowball engine, but it isn't. In fact, it is the opposite. Where games like Macao, Puerto Rico, and many more have the players construct little engines, Cuba goes in the other direction. It is all tactics, all scoring for the moment, and modifying the player sequence order to make sure that profitable shipping spaces and other shared game elements are available when [i]you[/i] want them to be available. This requires a completely different mode and style of play: one which is far more opportunistic. Now that is a perfectly legal way to construct a game; I just wonder whether such a mode of play has lasting appeal. It is hard to say why a player won a particular game: he just weaseled better than the others, that's about as far as you can go.

There are plenty of moments where you 'feel' the presence of the other players, however: this isn't a game where you can afford to keep busy with your own little playing field. At the same time, the game is sometimes frustrating in that moves which give you points are simply no longer available, requiring you to make what feels like sure suboptimal moves. My greatest source of frustration is that there are a [i]lot[/i] of buildings which appear to do absolutely nothing interesting at all. Take, for example, the Cement Factory. It costs 1 wood and 2 stone to build, and converts 4 stone into 4 VP. A player can only get 3 stone per turn: one from the tradeswoman (and then only using a specific action which is available to a single player per round only), two from his plantation board. But stone is required for other buildings (worth 2 VP at the end of the game), and at some point you may want to put your worker in a different spot, use the foreman in a different way, or use the tradeswoman in a different way. It doesn't at all sound like a good deal to me save for dotting a few i's. The same goes for money-making buildings. It takes quite a bit of effort to set up, and then gives you very little cash in return. Often you want money [i]now[/i] or in the next turn; not in three turns or so. The market is seldom interesting because the prices of goods are often too low to sell against an interesting price, and often too high for regular purchasing. And so forth. The more I play the game, the more I get irritated with the game's senseless friction which does nothing worthwhile save have every player hunting for scraps and morsels which turned up at some point; indeed, [i]weasling[/i] for all they're worth.

As a result, I've lowered my rating. There are good ideas lurking in Cuba, but they didn't come out nearly as well as I would have liked.

[b]Update[/b]   After playing for the first time in a long time (several years) I look upon this title with an air of [i]What in blazes had I been smoking when I bought it?[/i] (Which, considering that I don't smoke, takes some doing obviously.) I already nailed the gist of the main problems above: it's all about weasling points here and there. An exceedingly boring way of creating a game. Downgraded by two points, moved to the trade pile. Its successor Santiago de Cuba is so much more fun: it keeps the point weasling, but removes needlessly tacked on mechanisms which don't add anything save for causing friction and annoyed tempers. Plus it keeps the game light and accessible: Cuba itself pretends to be big and strategic, but it isn't.

RATING: 7.5
Cuba on the surface appears to be a typical worker placement game (collect goods, erect buildings, ship goods), but it adds a few interesting elements to set it apart.  Players select one of five roles to play each turn, with the last being reserved for voting in parliament.  That voting is influenced by bribes (essentially a silent auction), with the outcome setting new rules for the round.

What really makes the game shine is how everything is inter-related, so you really must choose the timing of your actions very carefully.  This is particularly crucial when adding buildings, which cover up spaces you would normally collect goods.

Not my favorite game in the genre, but unique enough to stay in our collection.

RATING: 7
Cuba has a great beautiful board with great artwork on the component tiles as well.  I liked the options that the building tiles gave me, and the decisions you have to make in covering tiles on your player board.  I also liked the concept of the action card select with the alternate options.

We played with the El Presidente expansion, which I liked.  This gave you a little more variability in taking the actions you needed.  

I don't have much of an opinion on the laws.  I did like that they were not overly harsh.  They added some flavor, which was nice.

The thing I did not like was how the action selection cards worked out in the game.  Nearly every player turn started with the same action.  Then the shipping part usually played out one of two ways each turn.  So that means that a good part of deciding what to do each turn is too similar from turn to turn.  

So this reduced the number of decisions I had available making the game too light in my opinion.  The game was solid, but it was just missing something for me hook me.

There is a lot to like, so I would like to play it again.  I think there is a lot of replay with the way the buildings work.  It has some good puzzle moments, but the cards are a let down.

I don't think the game is as heavy as BGG lists, though.  It's a good game, and more accessible than the weight would imply.

RATING: 8
I've played this 3 times now and have thoroughly enjoyed it every time. 

There are a lot of mechanics and therefore a lot of rules but it is well worth working through them.

The variety of mechanics mean that every turn is filled with individual puzzles but the competition for the market, buildings, shipping and for political power mean that there is a huge amount of player interaction.

The game is always very satisfying and feels grand in it's proportions but (rules explanations aside) never seems to drag and has very little downtime.

Always willing to play.

The spacial sub-game that takes place on your player board creates interesting decisions and there's the element of figuring out how to play your character cards in the right order to 1) maximize the engine building, and/or 2)beat your opponents to parts of the board. Sometimes those objectives conflict leading to more fun decision making.

Good game with a good theme and great art and pieces that is somewhat underrated as an intro game.

RATING: 6
Fun game.  Played one 5er game.  The game tends to reward long-term planning.  I liked the resource generation, building purchases/activations, bills.  The one thing I didn't care for was how bills were passed.  The blind bidding thing where you lose your bid is pretty harsh.  Also, it was not obvious what happens when there are ties and no one wants to bid to break ties.  I'd probably rate this a 7, but I expect replayability to be low.  There are only a few bill cards...they are all going to see play every game.  The game does seem to reward a single-minded strategy, not offering much opportunity to switch tracks.  And if you wind up sharing the tracks w/another player, it will impact your score in a bad way.

RATING: 6
Players are commodity dealers in early Cuba, trying make their fortunes and influence legislation.  Some proposed laws are flipped up, players take turns taking actions to acquire, process, and dispose of stuff, then laws are voted on.  Repeat this for six turns.  Behind this lofty description we have a fairly routine cube mincer - take actions, manipulate stuff, and spit out victory points.  Who can do it the best wins.  Looks glorious, and is mildly interesting, but feels like it's on the coattails of many other games that do it better.  Not bad by any means, but not that great either.  Feels a bit long, and like they forgot to add the fun factor.

RATING: N/A
After my first play, I quite like the game. The game has been on my wishlist for the longest time. The only thing holding me back from getting it is the parliament/politics.

I may have overjudged the game a little, as the voting phase at the parliament is not even difficult to understand.

I really like the part where when you build a building, it actually covers the production space. The decision to pick which production space to be permanently covered (thus stopping it's production) is quite critical, and I like that part.

Components are great (the game us known for that), especially the rum bottle.

The variable phase order is quite interesting nevertheless. A little twist from Puerto Rico, and I like both styles.

RATING: 6
SOLD IT!

A lighter version of other similar games. There is less of a "whoops, I made a single bad decission and will now be at a serious disadvantage" feel that I get from games like Caylus, and more of a "well, I'm making some generaly good decissions, so I will have a good shot at wining" feeling. This could be a good or bad thing depending on what kind of game you prefer.

I will say that I always feel like I have done very little by the end of a game session. With only 30 actions taken per player (6 rounds times 4 actions and 1 voting/resolution step) you will often take an entire round to simply make one "play". What I mean is that my turn might be; position a worker, get a resource, sell it, and trade it for a victory point. The kind of thing that would happen MANY times in a similar game and not just a few times in this one. I understand that longer game would only see your economic engine repreat itself, but I would prefer a more satisfied end game feel.

RATING: 8
I think I can start this off as an 8.  Not higher, because it's really just a rehash of mechanics that have already been sucessful in other games. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A pale imitation of Puerto Rico, this game borrows the plantation player board, the idea of choosing roles which determine player actions, the concept of shipping goods on vessels with restricted cargo capacities, and the construction of buildings which give players special privileges or advantages; unfortunately, its designers didn't duplicate Puerto Rico's tense decision making, interesting player interaction, or engaging theme.  Even the Caribbean island theme seems lifted from Puerto Rico.

The game does offer some worthwhile innovations, particularly the clearing of spaces on the plantation boards to erect buildings, forcing a player to sacrifice resource production for development, the mechanism for choosing the first player and the idea of the Statutes (which can affect victory point computation and which can be enacted by purchased votes) are mildly interesting. 

On the whole, the game strikes me as a mediocre copy of Puerto Rico, emulating many successful attributes of its inspiration yet lacking the genius that makes the original great.  Truly, the similarities between this game and Puerto Rico are so pronounced as to make it appear to be a shameless copy with just enough changes made to avoid a lawsuit.  An OK game, rated 5 by me and by my wife, but IMHO, you'd be wise to buy Puerto Rico and forget about Cuba.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Quality game & game play.  Beautiful board, well made pieces.  Rules clear & well written.  The board is laid out so that most play is obvious and referring to rules is kept to a minimum.  This game has elements of lots of other games, but the combination creates unique play.  I especially appreciate the mechanics that when personal supply areas (crops, mines, etc.) are built upon, your crop yield and building material production is reduced and must perhaps be purchased, but supply and demand realistically regulates prices.  Influence of town officials is based upon voting and can play an important game factor.  Many approaches to winning this game.

RATING: 7
A bit long for what it is, but everything works nicely together and feels fresh despite what so many comment about it being derivative.  My only problem is in what I feel are somewhat limited strategies with regard to the buildings.  Since you can only activate so many buildings in a single column/row and so many of the buildings are so obviously paired with another building, you often don't have the luxury of choice once you proceed down one of the obvious 6 or 7 paths.  You can tweak it a little bit, but not much.  That's not a big deal because the game is all about the interaction of the players with the bills, market, and shipping, but if you really had some flexibility with the buildings it would take the game to the next level.  So many of them are so inferior that they never get bought except in the most specific of situations.   

My comments for this one still stand, but I've had to lower my rating since my tastes have changed quite a bit since I last played it.

RATING: 9
Yes, it is once again about resource management but this game has some of the best elements of my favourite games.  It's the differences that are important; buildings will cover resources or produce preventing their harvesting, the actions are open to all players and the parliamentary element is a mechanism not used in any of the game's cousins.  I give this an 8 after one play; it's likely to rise from that.

UPDATE 20MAR08:  Definitely a keeper this one, and I'm upping my rating to a 9.  There are more choices to be made than at first meet the eye; do you sacrifice shipping in favour of political power, do you sell produce or ship it, do you build now or later, do you need money or points?  I have to wonder if there is a key strategy under all this, but as it stands there seem to be many varied routes to victory.

RATING: 7
Yet another resource conversion game that cobbles together many known mechanics. Does that make it derivative? I don't think so. Even though most of the individual parts are familiar from other games Cuba does have its own vibe. The resource production on the plantation, turn order constraints imposed by the role selection, vying for control in parliament, shipping goods, constructing buildings for increased leverage; all these elements have their place and work together pretty well.

My main concern is replayability. Despite the large number of buildings available there is a very small number of obvious paths to go down. You can make a few adjustments as you go along, and much of the game is a lot more tactical than it is strategic, but the general direction is set very early, and there is not a lot of choice. I'm curious to see how that will turn out after a few plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've played this a couple of times now (even before logging my plays) and it's always a nice experience. Biggest problem I have with this game is that there are a lot of buildings and your strategy is dependend on which building you'll want to get.

Next time I'm using a 'cheat sheet' for the building.

RATING: 7.5
After a single five player game I can attest to the complexity, beauty, and cleverness of this game design.  But it is in no way a better game than Puerto Rico, a game it tries hard to improve upon.  It doesn't and it doesn't because of the "gaminess" of the Worker/Foreman mechanism on the player's boards which requires plantations (products) and resources to be replaced by buildings.  I'm not excited about the blind bidding mechanic that determines which bills will pass into law.  For my money, Puerto Rico scratches the same itch much better. 

After a 2nd play I'm increasing my rating to an 8.  

Picked up a copy for 10 bucks from a member of my game group and sold it back to him for 15 - I had enhanced the game with a plastic organizer.

RATING: 7
Not too heavy, not too light - Cuba is a great middle-of-the-range eurogame. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a re-hashing of several recent euros, but thats not a bad thing.  I had fun playing the game, though I think its too long for this type of game, there is too much downtime, not enough interaction, and although there are several different strategies to explore, none of them are really very interesting.  The game looks great and has a very strong theme, and is great as a "play once every few years" type of eurogame.  If there was some way to do something to the other players, the game would benefit a lot.  Also, several of the buildings were so atrocious I can't see buying them.  I don't want to knock the game too much because I think it is fun, thematic (for a euro), well-balanced and smoothly designed.  There are multiple distinct strategies you can take in the game, and most of them seem pretty viable.  The market mechanics are interesting but in our game they didn't do much.  Players who had played more than me said they had never seen the market have a big effect throughout the game, it is more situational.  The role selection mechanic was a rehash, and a poor one.  Since each player had their own roles, it lessened interaction.  We started playing our entire turns without waiting for the other players because the interaction was so low, and downtime so high, it seemed the natural course of action.  Normally, I would give any game with rum and cigar tokens a 10, but this one really wasn't up to snuff.  It looks so promising, but ultimately, its a turn the wheel make the VPs pop out game.  A solid design, great components, good theme, but low interaction, high downtime, and ultimately shallow gameplay killed this one for me.

RATING: 7.5
I enjoyed this title, which appears to be a well balanced mix of mechanics from other games.  Each player has the same 5 "roles", of which he/she will play 4, the 5th being used for the voting phase of the game.

One of the "roles" collects resources/produce for the player.  Another role allows for the  building/purchase of building tiles, similar to Caylus.  Players score points when they deliver goods to the boats at the dock (value of delivery increases the longer the board stays in port).

The 5th "role", which was not played, now acts as the number of votes the player has for the Political Phase, where 2 out of 4 new laws are selected by the winner of the vote "bid" (pesos can be added to bonify your "offer").

All in all, good game length, clear mechanic, has multiple paths to victory (via tile combos, different strategies, etc.).  IMHO, way ahead of PR.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
JEM 13.szám: http://jemmagazin.hu/magazin/page/2/

Minden játékosnak van egy saját ültetvénye,ahol építőanyagokat (követ, fát és vizet) termelhet ki, és nyersanyagokat(citrust,cukornádat és dohányt)termeszthet. Az építőanyagok segítségével különböző épületek építhetők,amiket szintén a saját ültetvényünkön kell felhúzni.Az így elfoglalt területet már nem használhatjuk eredeti rendeltetésére, így gondosan meg kell terveznünk, hogy melyik építőanyag vagy nyersanyagforrásunkat áldozzuk fel egy épületért és az előnyökért. A nyersanyagok eladhatók pénzért a piacon, vagy behajózhatók a megfelelő igénnyel rendelkező hajóra, és ezáltal győzelmi pontokat lehet szerezni. Ezen kívül a megfelelő épületek segítségével átalakíthatók árucikkekké: a cukornádból rumot, a dohányból szivart lehet előállítani. Ha nincs megfelelő épületünk, akkor ezeket a piacon is megvásárolhatjuk. Az árucikkek nem érnek többet, mint a nyersanyagok (csupán a piacon a kezdeti áruk magasabb), de a behajózási lehetőségen kívül egyes épületek segítségével is beválthatók győzelmi pontra.

 A központi táblán található a Parlament, ahol a játék egyik egyediségét adó törvénytervezeteket szavazzák meg. Minél tovább várakoznak a kikötőben a hajók, annál több pontot ér a megfelelő áru berakodása.A játék 6 fordulóból áll, amelyek mindegyikében a játékosok 4-4 akciót hajthatnak végre. Az elérhető 5 lehetséges akciót 5 különböző szerepkártya segítségével lehet végrehajtani, amelyekből minden játékos birtokol egyet. Egy körben az aktuális játékos kijátszik egy, abban a fordulóban általa még nem használt lapot, és végrehajtja a rajta lévő akciót. Amikor minden játékos végrehajtott 4 akciót, az akciófázis véget ér. A megmaradt 5. lapokat a törvénytervezetekre szavazásnál lehet majd használni.

A behajózás szinte minden esetben létfontosságú. Úgy tűnik, ezzel az akcióval lehet a legkönnyebben győzelmi pontot szerezni, de ehhez nagyon fontos az időzítés. Figyelnünk kell az épületek megfelelő leépítésére, hiszen ha jól raktuk le őket, egyszerre többet tudunk használni. 
Természetesen pénzre is szükségünk lesz, áru vásárlásra és a törvények befolyásolásához. 

Elsőre megfogott a játék. Van akinek nagyon tetszik, van akinek nem. Én az első tábort erősítem, ezért is szereztem be, bár messze nem játszok vele annyit, mint amennyit szeretnék.

RATING: 7
This game has tons of strategy and the rules are simple.  The game looks great and is of high quality.  So why don't I give this game a 9 or 10?  Because I don't find it that fun.  There isn't a sense of rising action, the 6 turn game seems arbitrary... why not 5 or 7?  I have played it 3 times in 2 player mode and once with 4 people.  The game is definitely better the more people you have as voting becomes a real issue and there is more stomping over people's strategy (aka someone bought the building you needed or loaded a boat full of stuff).

However the game does end with no chance of stretching out from 2 hours to 3 or 4 which is great.

If you like the feeling of winning a chess game, you will like Cuba, because Cuba is a thinking man's game and not a pretzles and beer affair.

RATING: 7
Well, this is very Euro. I wouldn't really call it a worker placement game since it doesn't have an action drafting aspect to the game--what people have referred to as worker placement is just a rather specialized way of deciding what you will produce on your (privately owned) land that round.  

I enjoy the tension involved in putting things on boats and trying to predict when they will launch.  To me, that's the most interesting thing about the game, though I also enjoy the way the harvest works.  I'm not especially excited about the goods market or the laws though. I don't know, it seems like I'm anatomizing the game here because I haven't played it enough to think of it as a whole. (3 plays)

RATING: N/A
Parece inevitable compararlo con Puerto Rico (PR). Tomando ideas de otros juegos de gestión de recursos, de elección de roles y de gestión de la mano, consiguen hacer un juego con relativo éxito que parece despreciado por el sector duro de la afición precisamente por este préstamo de ideas. Me gusta la mecánica de la votación, según la cual aparecen nuevas leyes que afectan a aspectos del juego (impuestos, subsidios, mercado) y los jugadores votarán con su rol no jugado en la fase (algunos roles tienen más votos que otros). PROS: No afectas tanto al de la izquierda (fallo de Puerto Rico que aquí no se repite). No hay necesidad de memorizar los efectos de los edificios como en Puerto Rico (son autoexplicativos al mirar sus losetas). Versión más "extreme" de Puerto Rico. CONS: Puerto Rico tiende a construir un "motor de PV´s", pero Cuba parece más bien al contrario (hacer el máximo de PV´s en cada momento), lo que le resta profundidad y le hace ser más "oportunista". Hay edificios que parecen no servir para nada como "la cementera" (4 piedras para conseguir 4 PV... pero según las reglas, sólo se pueden conseguir 3 piedras por turno: una de la comerciante y dos por la plantación)

RATING: 7
This game has some familiar vibes, alot of people say its like puerto rico. I disagree, yes you can ship goods for victory points, but there are so many other choices to be made that also get victory points. You have player matts infront of you, but its not just Puerto Rico that uses a player matt. You use roles but these are determined by you, everyone gets the same 5 and uses them in which order they like.

There isnt much interavtion save for an election at the end of each of the 6 rounds. I didnt like the fact the game was over before I could develop a suitable strategy. Having to save enough cubes - 2 or 3 rounds to use a building potentialy 3 times and thats if you want 1 building the second is realised for goes 5 and 6.

I personally felt the game this is closest too is GOA, if you enjoyed that then you will love this.

RATING: 7.5
Fun game.  Comparisons between Cuba and Puerto Rico are fair (e.g., the picking of roles in each turn, producing goods, shipping and selling of goods, buying buildings that provide benefit); however, this is its own game.

Key differences: 
 - The passage of two new "laws" each turn can dramatically influence the game's short-term decision making and improves the game's replayability (i.e., make it less "scripted" than Puerto Rico can be sometimes).
 - Victory points are public.
 - Each player selects 4 (of 5) roles to use each turn and other player's don't receive a benefit from your role decisions.

[Those being noted, given that it does have similarity to PR - which I own - and the game collection is fairly robust at this point, Cuba is just not so unique thematically / mechanically to place it where it otherwise would be on the 'next games to order' list.]

RATING: 7
It's been compared to many, but in fact it reminds _Caylus_ the most.  All these buildings, their abilities and combinations of them...  Role selection is not a new concept either...  But Cuba is original enough to value it on its own.  

This is actually very very solid game in 'resource management & converting them into VPs' area.  It has good potential - several strategies truly exist.

Acts in the parliament is an innovative mechanics.  It has perfectly balanced impact on the game - not too small and not too big.  Great job.

EDIT: Oops...  After 11 plays I'm starting to have a problem with the replayability (even with 'El Presidente' expansion)... :(

RATING: 6
Pretty derivative game which  only fares so so against those it borrows from. Less random than Pillars but much more than PR or Caylus. 

Being a 2 hour strategy game I thought the level of randomness and chaos was a little too high here. I am talking about the blind bidding to determine who enacts the laws, and of course the laws do come up randomly as do the ships.  Now normally I don't mind blind bidding but I have a couple problems with how it was implemented here. Everyone loses their money whether they win or not, this just makes the chaotic nature of the blind bidding worse as if you lose even by a dollar you throw all the money you bid away?  Which leads into my next point, second place in the auction should possibly get an action too.  Really I think these laws are far too important to be left up to blind bidding and maybe having money secret with a regular auction might be a better way to resolve this phase? It works fine as is but I'm personally not very keen on it as there is too little control. 

Although I have only touched on the negative aspects I do think the overall game is good but nothing special to be sure, 'just another euro' a most fitting description. Not a game I need to own, occasional play maybe to explore the multiple paths maybe but I won't be requesting it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Cuba is a good game (and an undeniably gorgeous production), but exactly how good remains to be seen.

Each player has an individual board with the potential to produce three types of commodity: "resources" (stone, water, wood), "products" (tobacco, citrus fruit, sugar cane), and "goods" (cigars, rum). Resources are used either for construction or, in the case of water, to occasionally squeeze extra production from your fields. Products may be shipped at the harbor for points or bought and sold at market (possibly generating some cash). "Goods" are not actually more valuable (a natural expectation given their position in a production-chain), but they can be vertically integrated into a player's strategy. In other words, "goods" and the associated scoring opportunities are subject to less competition than "products." An odd distinction, perhaps, but certainly a fresh one in games of this genre.

Cuba's foundation is sound, and the designers went to some lengths to build several levels of replayability on top of it. Most prominent of these are the much-touted "laws," which create new scoring opportunities, remove old ones, favor poor or rich players, and tamper with the supply and demand of resources and products at market. They appeal to me in concept but seem curiously tame in practice, and their passage (2 per round) involves a surprising amount of risk. Players who wish to purchase sway in the Cuban Parliament participate in a blind bid, but even the losers pay up. I can't figure out the design intent behind this, as it mitigates the cost for the winner and exaggerates it for the losers.

Player actions in Cuba walk a fine line between juicy tension and dull constraint. Each round, players choose four of their five possible actions, but there are some very strict ordering issues for at least the first half of the game. Ideally, I would want to see multiple good reasons to order your actions differently. Instead it seems like there is surprisingly little to take into account when deciding what order to play your cards in. On the other hand, Cuba contains a novel mechanism for producing commodities and activating buildings. Each player board contains a 3x5 grid of fields, lakes, quarries, forests, and buildings, but players can only activate one 'cross' (intersecting row/column) on the grid. This makes players think carefully about building placement, constrains perfect/efficient output, and mandates specialization, which is further encouraged by the number of unique buildings available for construction.

Cuba is a beautiful game with a lot of charm. There's a lot that looks intriguing but perhaps not the follow-through to match. I enjoyed it and would happily play again 2-3 more times to form a more concrete impression, but I'm not sure it has legs. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Second game was not so good. Again, three of us were close together until the final round but a decision by the fourth player regarding port legislation gave points to the two others and destroyed my chances for the win. What use squeezing out any possible VP when a few points randomly thrown to somebody decide in the end.  
---
Three of us ended with 73 VP and were only 1 gold apart. I experienced such a close end only once before in a multiplayer game with two-digit end scores. It was La Strada and that's a game where decisions don't matter too much. I'm not sure if Cuba suffers from the same arbitrariness or if we just were too opportunistic and not focussed enough. In any case, Cuba is one of these games where each single VP has to be squeezed out. With experienced thinky players Cuba will suffer from AP but can shine in the planning department of setting up your turns for the round. Which role card do I want to keep for the lawmaker phase, in what order do I play the remaining actions, how do I use each single action. Truly multidimensional decisions, hopefully they matter with more experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm not quite sure what to make of this game. It reminded me of "Pillars of the Earth" the first time I encountered it. Perhaps this is because it was a Rieneck/Stadler design. I think the 6 fixed rounds, the market, the converting cubes to victory points, and the flat economic engine all reminded me of Pillars. But to other people, this design reminded them more of Puerto Rico.

After the first game, I thought this was a better "Pillars." After the second game, I wasn't too sure. After the third game, I'm leaning toward liking it slightly better than Pillars.

I think both games suffers from being a bit too long for what it is. The last  game of Cuba I played probably lasted about 2.5 hours including explanation. I would feel better playing a game like this if it was less than 90 minutes (and I guess it could still fall into that time if people are experienced, but Pillars has never really drastically reduced in playtime).

Both Pillars and Cuba looks like a "build your economic engine" type of game, but they are rather "flat" economic engines. In Pillars, the ramping up of the VP points is due to the introduction of more powerful craftsmen. In Cuba, you are building buildings, but each one you build will shut down one of your other production, so the effect of the buildings is damped by the loss of the production. Also in Cuba, the range of the building powers is quite small, they all give you incremental improvements, but none are earth shattering.

One thing I liked about this game was that it was able to accomodate 5 players. However, the game is quite expensive, even coming from Rio Grande.

RATING: 7
Base Game: Fun worker placement game with some role selection elements and personal building boards. Several paths to victory, but some can get hosed if someone else beats them to a needed building for maximization of the path selected. 3-4 player works best for base game, although with 3, there is often a lot of room for shipping.

Cuba - El Presidente expansion: [rant]Price is ridiculous for this expansion.[/rant] The additional buildings, ship cards and law cards are all a nice addition to add some variety and other game strategies (especially for 5-players. The New Cuban cards and presidential action add a little bit of randomness to the game. Overall, not a bad addition, but not worth the MSRP.

RATING: 5
Resource management game with a blend of very familiar mechanisms but the result is not very original. Optimisation of the role selection is interesting and building the combo of tiles to generate victory points is useful. The ships and their different cargoes will ensure variability. Although I like the idea of the voting, this mechanism also makes it very difficult to plan ahead. There are also lots of ways to get victory points, which gives the impression that it doesn't really matter what you do (even a combo isn't really a must). Game play is way too long with 5 players. In the end, the first play was rather underwhelming and a bit disappointing. Confirmed by a second play - nothing spectacular.

RATING: 7
The components were very nice, especially the gorgeous board (which was a lot more functional than [GAMEID=24480]).  The wooden bits reminded us a lot of [GAMEID=31260], as well as the personal plantation mats.  However, [GAMEID=30380] doesn't hold back---it immediately throws you into the full game with all 25 buildings and new laws.  It was almost too much to take in and I wish that there as an intro game setup.  However, everything started to click towards the end of our first game.  There seem to be quite a few different strategies, with many different interacting resources and "engines" that you could develop.  On the other hand, in the long term I wonder if there's enough variety by changing up just the ships and order of the laws each game (will people tend to buy the same buildings each game), but that's something that we'll have to see.

On the plus side, it definitely felt like there was a lot of real decisions to make.  The one thing that it tries to capture from [GAMEID=31260] is the [i]tension[/i]---it feels almost way too short to set up your engine and get it running in only six turns.  On the other hand, the tax/harvest phase is not as intense as the [GAMEID=31260] harvest since the penalty doesn't feel as severe.  Moreover, with just two players the gameplay feels too wide open and non-interactive.  Particularly with two players, there's not too much competition with each other (e.g., it's easy for both players to ship and to buy buildings)---there may be tension over deciding your strategy, but there's not much tension in fighting the other players.  As a result, we've had a couple of runaway leader games where there was not much that the losing player could do to affect the other once they made a mistake that set them back.

In addition, while we enjoyed it, it didn't elicit an automatic "we [i]need[/i] to play this again" vibe like we got from [GAMEID=31260].  Although we found the game pleasant, it's likely to be much better with more players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Yet another 'mechanism in search of a game'. Luckily this one works nicely. Except the rulebook is a lazy translation involving poor grammar, poor word choices, poor organization (including the non-English alphabetical ordering) and inconsistent naming of the same thing using different phrases in different places; blech!

NOTE: Don't forget to put your buildings "in the appropriate places on and near the board". Did anyone read these random words before publication?

We're not the only ones who couldn't tell the difference between a 'playing piece' and a 'playing marker'; no one could think of 'worker' and 'score marker'?

An English translator for the English rules... There are comments online that show even company reps didn't understand the rules when demoing the game.

[c]     [/c]I tried so hard, really I did. This one just annoys the PECOS out of me each and every time we bring it out (anyone reading this can probably tell, heh). My game aids help a lot...

RATING: 6
A great role selection game that is reminiscent of Puerto Rico but with corruption.  Each player has the same roles each turn and can execute them in different orders.  You can build buildings, ship goods and acquire resources.  Then there is a law phase which can changes the rules and scoring in the game.  

I love this game.  It is very under-rated, but you MUST have the El Presidente expansion.  The expansion makes this a very good game.

RATING: 7
[b]First impression of the game?[/b]
Seems like a good middle-weight game. 

[b]What are the highlights?[/b]
I like the selection system using the grid coupled with the role cards. The artwork and components are gorgeous.

[b]What are the drawbacks?[/b]
The grid system can be a little overwhelming with the choices available which leads to a tendency for some AP.
There is nothing earth-shattering from a game design perspective.

[b]Who would enjoy this game?[/b]
People who like a middle-weight Euro game. 

[b]Who would [i]not[/i] enjoy this game?[/b]
People who like to blow stuff up and people prone to AP.

[b]Would you play this game again?[/b]
I would very much like to play this again, possibly with my family.

Maybe harder for beginner boardgamers due to the tough decisions, but one or two games and you are all set.

RATING: 6.5
Bought through BGG Marketplace.

On its own Cuba, is a solid game.  The Puerto Rico similarities are really only superficial.  While you do ship in both games, Cuba has alternate win conditions (i.e. rum cafe/distillery, manipulating laws, etc.) that make it such that one can almost ignore shipping completely.  The role selection is also very different.

Rather, Cuba is more like Caverna, where it is a sandbox where you are free to try different strategies at your leisure.  The worker placement mechanic also requires strategic positioning, as adding buildings only decreases the resources you can get.

At 2 players, the 5 rounds feel fairly short.  It's also widely apparent that the 3VP ship is overpowered and that shipping is the only real strategy.  Our fix was to remove the 3VP ship.  Together with the El Presidente Expansion, the game comes up to a solid 7.5.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[i]Cuba[/i] feels like a less complicated version of [i]Puerto Rico[/i], and yet, I find it more enjoyable for the following reasons:

- There is no need to memorize the function of each building (the tiles explain everything very clearly)
- The mechanic used to determine first player and number of votes is unique
- Bribery!
- Strategic placement of buildings is required
- You have to plan ahead for each phase, making sure to play your cards in the correct order for maximum benefit

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
 A fusion of Puerto Rico, Caylus, and a number of other games I like.  Players must obtain resources by working farm areas on their own player board to get resources to buy buildings and goods to get victory points.  This game is filled with difficult choices and tradeoffs, as (for instance) when a player buys a building, they must cover up a production space on their board to place it.  The one downside is that there are about 50,000 different buildings, and resources are limited enough that it seems as though picking a strategy requires a good knowledge of all the buildings (as some make excellent combinations).  This is a neat game with multiple tracks that lead to victory, but I'm worried that it will have a steep learning curve, and that experienced players will always have a major advantage over inexperienced ones.

RATING: 6
Cuba looks great. I'm glad Eggert-Spiele is able to do something this cool - this looks like a Kosmos game.

What I like about Cuba: the six-round limit makes the game nice and tight. The decisions can be tough, and doing something means you're not doing something else.

I don't like the way the game can drag. I've played a five-player game that took 2.5 hours. That was painful. With three, I've been able to play the game in less than an hour. That's much better.

I find it hard to pinpoint what's the reason, but even though I think Cuba works, it isn't great fun to play. I have a feeling that with more experience and skill, the game becomes more interesting, but that's always a problem - why should I bother to play this more to see if I like the game, when I can play something else that I know I like.

Cuba is, however, interesting enough to make it possible. At least I'm rambling about it here a lot...

Indifferent.

Update:

I had played [thing=30380][/thing] once in 2009 where I wasn't overly impressed and again in 2013 where it went over a little better. I wasn't totally blown away, but I didn't feel as though I wasted my time either. To me [thing=30380][/thing] is something of the poster child for an 'okay euro', not one that overly excites but one that is okay and offers a few decent mechanics, some interest choices and has reasonable production values.

There it would have probably stood if it were not for a Sydney based gamer looking to get a bunch of people to group-purchase [thing=30380][/thing] from a local (to him) retailer for AUD$20 each. Well, it sounded like a pretty good deal so after a little hesitation I joined in. I was thinking that the game would make a handy birthday present to Mrs Fish from the Little Fish and event proved this to be true.

Anyway, we tried it out with two players last month and it went over fairly well.  She enjoyed the game and I enjoyed playing with her so that's all for the best.

Hopefully next time we'll get this on the table with more than two. So, worth trying again; I hear it is better with the expansion.

RATING: 6
Does this game replace Puerto Rico for me? I think so because of one main reason: control! Unlike Puerto Rico, you can choose your roles yourself to fit your strategy but must still take into account factors like the other players' choices, the market prices, the incoming ships, the current laws etc. Additional balance decisions come from which land to sacrifice for a building and which role to refrain from to vote. Add to that simple rules and gorgeous art and you have a perfect game for a summer evening with a glass of rum.

RATING: 8
A beautiful game, set in Havanna before the Revolution.  Each player is a wealthy family, and although pesos can be traded in the local market and for legislative power, foreign money is the goal for VPs.
Theme counts for a lot in this game. Cigars and rum sit at the top of the production chain, but production only matters if you want it to. There are so many buildings, and each build develops your engine while denying that option to others - but your engine might count for nothing if you can't deliver to the ships destined for export markets and the lucrative US$ cleverly disguised as VPs. Alternatives exist, such as taking control of the legislature for which local pesos will buy the votes you need.
The game plays fluidly with quick turns and little downtime. My only gripe is the end-game can feel jarringly arbitrary. Sometimes that's a blessing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Only played once, and you can read my session review on the BGG Cuba Sessions forum. I like the variety of things you can do in this game. You have 5 cards you can play every round, but you only select 4 of those. So unlike Puerto Rico, you can choose your Role Selection, and you can often change it depending on what other players do. There's so much going on in the board, yet it was easy to follow, and everything interacted so well. You can build/farm for resources, buy/sell at the market to get money/resources and then you could go to the harbor and ship resources for points, and while doing all this, there's a set of "laws" which can do things like tax or prevent certain things.

This balance of things is pretty cool, and I really appreciated it. The blind auction bribery to the government was a nice thematic touch, and I liked the variety of buildings - I also certainly could see multiple strategies popping up. (Though for games with loads/all of the buildable options out at once, I sometimes wonder if having a subset out would be ideal for replayability and not having a groupthink main strategy)

Slight issues with the game: The players who fell behind early stayed behind as it was an engine game (once you've got a good engine, you stay ahead), so they were just dragged for a 2 hour game that should've ended for them an hour ago. This isn't in every game of it, but I've definitely seen it. I think this could be fixed with more hidden VPs, not a blatant VP track where you can see the players lagging 12+ points behind. Also, the game's board can feel a bit fiddly/cramped with all the resources and things happening, but this is a minor complaint. It can be a bit luck based which ships come out too, so I wish there was a way to bribe the control of that or something. Also, the shipping theme isn't fun to me.

Overall, nice little game, and I definitely like it more than Puerto Rico, I think.

Plays: 2

NOTE TO SELF: Had been considering buying this, but according to what I read, its replayability is low ish, so I will wait and see on this. The expansion adds some variety, alongside the taxes, different ships, and different player interaction, so I may reconsider getting this more strongly in the future.

SECONDARY NOTE TO SELF: okay, after a lot of meditation, I decided not to get this since I prefer low downtime and high strategy so PR just does this role selection better, and its much tighter. The no downtime in PR is huge IMO.

[color=#117bc0][b]I'd probably rather play:[/b][/color] Puerto Rico
----
reacquired two years later. why? compulsive spending
----
3 (?) years later, finally got a 2nd play in, and I liked it better (but I'm also a different player vs when I first started out). The choices engage, and there is a tension in timing things relative to turn order, with much of the tension resolution coming from exploiting opportunities to flip the script (e.g., saving up for a particular parliament phase, playing the unexpected action card). 4 vs 5 is a big deal; 5 drags. There's a fair bit of AP for a simultaneous selection game. And perhaps some devastating combos, but you can only have one, maybe two. Therein lies the rub: Cuba's tone feels dated; choices come from poverty, with foreman and worker actions in particular neutralizing each other to some extent, and not enough room to do all that much. Contrast this to the contemporary euro feel of abundance in games such as Steam Time or Orleans: so many choices, each better than the last!

RATING: 6.75
規則:
*將25個建築隨機開著擺5*5，第一排放在圖板右側，三個原料放在市場6/5/4格上，兩個貨物放在6/5上(資源:木/石/水，原料:水果/糖/菸草，貨物:.酒/雪茄盒，後兩者算商品)，15張船卡洗勻開兩張放在1/2分區，並開一張在圖版左側邊緣，法令卡洗勻分成四堆放在圖板上緣，I起始為2元稅II為橘子，隨機起始拿起始卡
*每個玩家拿一張個人圖版跟顏色標記(方塊計分0，人放在個人圖版倉庫上)並拿10元，自選兩個資源跟兩個原料，5張腳色卡
*遊戲進行六輪，每輪五個階段，最後分高獲勝
A 法案 : 開法令卡每疊開最上面一張
B 行動 : 每人輪流出牌做行動，直到每個人出四張，最後一張票數最多的為下輪起始，平手越晚出牌的成為起始
> 工人 移動個人圖板工人棋子到任意格，並收成行列上所有資源，原料一次只能收兩個，可棄掉水資源來收超過兩個，資源放在最上面
>商人 可買賣任意數量的商品，如果市場沒有可用7元買，市場滿了可用3元賣，或是拿供應區任意資源或任意原料(必須是目前最便宜的)，此選項一回合只能被使用一次
*替代行動使用時拿黑色標記放圖版對應工人記錄之
>建築師 支付資源拿任意一棟建築放到個人圖板蓋掉任意格田，或是拿分，第一個拿2兩分，之後都1一分
>工頭 使用同行同列的所有建築物 或是 使用任一個建築物(使用倉庫則為儲存所有原料)
>市長 供應一艘船並拿分 或是 不上船拿錢，第一個4四元，之後2元
C 議會 : 每個人出最後張牌跟暗中出錢買票，所有出的錢都要棄掉，最多票的選兩張議案卡執行，效果持續到被蓋掉為止，沒被選的棄掉，平手在比一次，在平手靠近起始的贏
D 法令 : 由I~IV執行所有目前的法令，I跟II分別是付錢跟資源拿2分，如果兩個都拿到在加1分，III根據狀態拿分，IV特殊狀態卡
E 回合結束 : 所有沒存的原料棄掉，滿載的船移到船牌庫底，所有剩餘船往右補，並開新船直到圖版旁多一張，黑色標記移除，第二輪開始3號港的船一定會離港
*遊戲結束 每個建築物2分，平手比錢多
*所有資源有限，沒有就沒得拿
*變體 改為先決定要不出的牌蓋著，在輪流出4張牌

RATING: 7
A game that works and is well crafted but which I really struggle to enjoy. Firstly, there's a big learning curve with the buildings, meaning lots of downtime until you're familiar with it. Secondly, the decision on how best to place your buildings on your player mat, which will cover up and stop generation of resources and products, is agonizing. It seems no matter how much time you spend, you'll inevitably put it somewhere you'll later wish you hadn't, meaning you spend the rest of the game wishing for something else. There's a strong strategic element required to do well. You need to work how you're going to generate your VP's - shipping, building, what type of conversions, taxes, bills, etc - and how you're going to do them better than the others, usually through strong building combinations and optimal player mat placement. Both of these require serious downtime to get right. There's also a continual requirement for money, so as to influence how end-round taxes are to paid and which bonus cards will be in play, so the decisions on VP's vs money provide a great decision point. So the game has pluses and minuses - lots to think about, but too much downtime, and funnily enough, my in-game regrets end up diminishing overall enjoyment rather than making me feel like I need to play it again to get it right next time.

RATING: 8
La prima impressione è un déjà vu: un Puerto Rico riveduto e corretto ambientato ai tempi di Batista. La plancia personale, la collocazione geografica, le navi da caricare: tutto ricorda un gioco già visto.

Ma è solo un'impressione, le meccaniche sono completamente differenti, così come lo svolgimento del gioco. Cuba ti pone in ogni momento davanti a una scelta: godo del privilegio di un edificio e scelgo di rinunciare a una piantagione? Sposto i miei lavoratori per avere le mie risorse o li tengo nell'incrocio giusto per poi far attivare gli edifici che mi interessano? Invio le mie merci al porto o mi tengo buoni i voti del sindaco per vincere le elezioni e approvare le leggi che avvantaggeranno solo me? 

Non potendo costruire tutto, bisogna decidere cosa fare dei propri possedimenti, e il gioco fornisce svariate possibilità: produttore di materie prime, manifattore di beni di lusso, commerciante o politico cittadino. 

Ciliegina sulla torta - sia per meccaniche che per ambientazione - la fase delle leggi: il momento nel quale il giocatore che più ha "investito" se la gode e "si sistema" le leggi come meglio crede.

A tutto questo si aggiungono una dotazione e una qualità di materiali notevole e una grafica molto sopra la media.

Un bel gioco longevo e divertente, la cui scatola merita un posto nello scaffale a fianco del suo più illustre predecessore.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tentative rating after one play. Beautiful game of Puerto Rican descent. 

Our group really went separate ways in our initial impression of the game. Several negative voices where heard regarding play time (long), down time (insufferable), non inventive (Puerto Rico, Caylus plagiarism) and, the most serious complaint, too restricted.

I on the other hand kind of liked it. There's a lot of subsystems to digest (buildings, shipping, market) during the first play, which I believe accounts for concerns regarding both playing time and down time. The "nothing really new"-complaint I can fully understand, but as I'm not super fond of either Puerto Rico or Caylus this somehow seems to hit a sweeter spot (further plays will confirm or revoke this statement).

The "restricted" complaint I believe emanates from the basic premise of the game, which you either like or not. I see Cuba as a three step rocket: 

1. Early game (2 rounds) Pick a primary and a secondary strategy. Pursue the primary one. If thwarted by other player's actions (choice of buildings) or own mistakes (bad building placement) switch to secondary strategy 

2. Mid game (2 rounds) Perfect your engine. That is, build and trade according to the chosen strategy. It's now more or less to late to switch horses.

3. End game (2 rounds) Make the engine run at full speed. Try to squeeze as much juice (VP) out of your citrus fruits (or whatever your strategy is based on).

Based on this reasoning one can see that in Cuba one makes the grand choices and have the most freedom in the beginning of the game, just to have one's freedom more and more limited as the game draws to an end. During the end game, the choices should be more or less obvious. 

This leads to a very unfulfilling experience if you expect success = more choices (but remember Cuba is a communist society =) ): One'll end up looking for grand moves during the end game and get frustrated for not finding any. If one knows what to expect and works with the flow of the game, the experience will be improved (and playing time and down time will be shortened).

RATING: 6
First play: Beautiful artwork, components and gameplay provide a strongly themed time in the Caribbean. Many mechanics are familiar and engineered together for smooth, almost simultaneous play (unless you really want to follow what you opponents are up to): role selection and the order in which to carry out 1 of the 3 possibilities provides much of the decision angst, more so when the 1 role you can't play goes towards voting for control of rule breaking legislation which will hurt/benefit players. Such roles allow you to load ships for vps, or to dabble in the marketplace to sell and buy. One refreshing aspect is the activation of your plantation: you receive resources (for buildings) and produce (for shipping and the market) from the fields along row and column that your worker stands; erecting buildings on your plantation (to generate all kinds of production/vp/financial benefits) covers what you can harvest. So a game is laden with dilemmas and trade offs, and the brutal bribing for the votes can be anxious (if you lose you still cough up). But for my tastes there are not enough secrets on the island as all information is open, except for in-the-fist bids and what your opponents are thinking, so lacks the climatic reveal I like in most designs.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An okay second tier game with no wow factor, which immediately reminded me of Puerto Rico and Caylus, because major elements from those games have been lifted. However, Cuba is inferior to either of those great games.

The components are nice and overall it is not bad and I certainly will play it again. I suspect it will be more enjoyable after a few plays.

As in Puerto Rico, there is a role that lets a player ship goods for victory points. This role is called the "Mayor".  I guess calling it the "Captain" would have been a little too blatant!

The voting on Bills to become law is an interesting mechanic, but it is too small a factor to free the game design from the yoke of PR/Caylus duo.
