RATING: 6
Played with the [thing=40794][/thing] expansion, as was advised to me by lots of BGG reports.

This is a perfectly fine game that just didn't catch on with me and my group. 

The general idea is you start with a board full of production spots, and a hand of symmetrical action cards. Each player in turn chooses a card from their hand and resolves it's effects, or if playing with the expansion, you can resolve a minor effect from one of the cards in the display at the top of the board. Players use their action cards to produce, build a building on top of a field, activate their buildings, ship goods to boats, or buy/sell at the market.

The building tiles are quite limited. Having buildings gives you 2vp per building, so there's reason to have them, but only a few buildings convert lesser resources into greater ones, which means a big way the game expects you to get them is through buying and selling in the market. I like the tension of when a player chooses to play what card, and I like how in the end of the round, all but 1 of your cards will be played, but I've been spoiled by engine building games to think that I want my later turns to have built on previous ones, and that's just not the case here. You do however, have to be very aware of what other people have so that you don't set them up too much to get points.

An odd mechanism is that once the action phase is over, you bid for event cards to resolve. Players do a secret bid where everyone pays if they win or not, then you add the number on your unused card to tally your total votes. The winner gets to apply 2 of the 4 event cards to the event space, and then you resolve them from left to right. Money isn't used for much outside the market, so I guess it's ok to pay for a losing bid that has no effect, but it feels strange. I also imagine that some amount of prediction of what's likely to be selected, and then plan for that contingency is a key part of doing well at this game.

I think there's more that I could learn in terms of understanding this game, but the core experience wasn't very exciting, so I'm not inclined to put that time investment in. The game has an adjustable length from 6 to 8 rounds, depending on how many events you pack in, but what you do on your turns each round feels a little too samey to keep me invested.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:nostar: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:nostar: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Nice tight, well balanced, game of building, managing, trading, and shipping. Draws elements from other popular games and adds a twist to each.

Role selection from PR - but each player sets their own sequence

True supply & demand market

Shipping - Each ship takes a mix of goods and progresses over time to higher value and then leaves

Variable rules - votes via card and blind bid bribes to determine who selects/changes half the laws/taxes in that turn

Individual player matts - buildings go onto your resource squares and activation of buildings and collection of resources are based on placement of common marker which makes for a puzzle solving aspect to your own board (PR & PoF joined)

RATING: 6
A difficult game to play.  It is too easy for your plans to be undermined by the player before you. RATING: 8
Can a game with cigars & rum be all bad?

Definitely a blend of mechanics from several other games - but with its own flair. 
Has the competition to get the shipping - wait you get more points at the risk that somebody jumps in front.
Buildings help you process resources and can provide victory point paths not available to other players.
Has resource allocation - do you ship raw products or use them to make more finished goods.
A voting mechanic to change the costs and how certain victory points are awarded.
Each person has 4 different actions and 5 actions to choose.

Which ever path you choose, need to watch the other players so they don't choose a similar path.

The game looks to be vulnerable to the heavy analysis where you come up with the "perfect" plan
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a big, slightly messy, extremely lush board game with lots going on. It does feel somewhat derivative of other games, as has been noted elsewhere, but not necessarily in a bad way. There is an interesting tension going on as one is forced to cover over one's sources of basic resources with buildings as you get your victory point engine running. The different laws coming into play are a nice diversion. I especially like the use of the five various role cards. Each is quite handy each turn, but one must weigh when to use each one to its best effect. I am quite happy with this game.

RATING: 8
Another one of my favorites.  I only gave it an 8, but it becomes a solid 9 with the El Presidente expansion, which fills the few gaps from the base game.  I like to call Cuba a "sexier" version of Puerto Rico with a lot of different elements to it - bidding for votes, main actions, secondary actions, buildings, shipping.  Despite everything going on, it is very intuitive and easy to pick up.  Also, it has a 6 turn limit (bumped up to 8 with the expansion) so it isn't going to drag on forever.  Little bit of learning curve to get familiar with the buildings, but nothing major.  An excellent game that I look forward to getting on the table more in the future.

RATING: 4
Cuba (Rio Grande) -- Played twice; four-player.  

I [i][b]want[/b][/i] to like this game.  

After all, there are so many truly cool mechanisms involved.  To name just a few: the use of the un-played character card to augment votes; the changing of only two of four statutes per round, with the possibility that some may stay in effect throughout the game; and the decrease in production necessary to place a building, whose power still requires activation.  

The mechanics all fit together well.  The theme is incorporated throughout.  The artwork is quite pleasing.  And the components are top-notch.  

I find it easy to respect this game.  I just haven't managed to enjoy it.  It feels more like I'm trying to overcome built-in obstacles than like playing against the other players.  And perhaps most importantly, it doesn't feel like play.  It feels like work.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1.5
Final verdict time. Well maybe not yet. There is a tiny glimmer of hope, but here's what I got: 

This is the everything but the kitchen sink euro. Its supposed complexity is being born more of player's confusion trying to find their way out of this mish mash of mechanics than any actual depth. The game used to annoy me a bit, being a MPS and a confusing game in general. Then I left it for 2-3 years and we recently played it with other games from "cuba trilogy" and after many soulless euros this one had a certain charm and promised space to explore and strategies to pursuit. So I tried it solo to see this space. It turns out this is mostly a tactical game, trying to gain advantage of ships and voting in the parliament, [i]however the game's mechanics are far too opaque and needlessly convoluted for interaction between players being able to shine through[/i]. There are just too many pieces of this puzzle that are situational (depending on laws being in effect) and/or too arbitrary.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Cuba was a gift from my 2011 BGC Secret Santa, and I have played 10 games of it – with half of those plays being with the El Presidente expansion – before putting it up for sale with little regret. The best part of the game for me lies in the plantation grid. It’s a unique minigame of positioning the lone worker to activate resource slots and buildings, and there are tough trade-offs over which resource slots should be replaced. 

The rest of the game, however, is met with mixed feelings. I love how the law system captures the seedy undercurrents of politics, but the requirement for [i]all[/i] bids to be paid for is a huge disincentive for most to participate in the process. ([thing=96913][/thing] implements a similar law system more successfully by requiring majority votes for a law to pass.) There’s also a lot of visual clutter with 23 unique buildings crowding the table. Some of this clutter is unnecessary, given that a good third of these buildings is far too niche to see proper play. 

But my biggest beef with Cuba is that it’s just too long for what it is. It's a game of opportunistic plays – most of the VP gains are incremental, and there's minimal control over what goods are in demand. Ideally, such a game should wrap up quickly, but Cuba outlasts its welcome two-thirds through. Bummer. Even the cute rum bottles and Menzel’s sunny artwork can't salvage the middling gameplay from the shuffle of more current Euros.

RATING: 7
[2011-09-10]
Egy látatlanban beszerzett játék. Szabály alapján nagyon megtetszett, a kivitelezés pedig pazarnak tűnt. Ez utóbbi teljesen igaz a játékra. Minden eleme nagyon jól kidolgozott, tartós. A tábla gyönyörű, az épületlapok szépek. Szóval a kivitelezés szinte tökéletes. Egyedül talán a törvénylapok szövegei helyett lehettek volna ikonok a nyelvfüggetlenség jegyében, bár mivel kevés van belőlük nem nehéz megjegyezni. Földművelő gazdákat alakítunk Kuba szigetvilágán, akik épületeket építenek, terményeket termesztenek, ezeket esetleg árucikkekké alakítják, amiket behajóznak. Ezzel lehet győzelmi pontot szerezni, illetve a különböző funkciójú épületeket által. A játékosok egy kör alatt az 5 fajta szerepből 4-et választanak ki, amelyek meghatározzák az adott kör alatti cselekvésüket. A termények és építőanyagok megszerzése egy jól kigondolt mechanizmuson alapul. Gazdálkodó emberünkkel a 3x4-es saját területünkön az adott sorban és oszlopban levő földeken termelünk. A játék igazi újdonságát a politikai rész adja. Pénzbeli és terményadó, illetve valamilyen győzelmi pontot hozó törvény mellett a negyedik a piacot és egyéb dolgokat befolyásol. A törvényekből kettőt a legtöbb szavazattal rendelkező játékos változtatja meg a forduló végén. Ezek a rendeletek nagyban befolyásolják a játék menetét és megszerezhető győzelmi pontokat így nagyon fontos kinek sikerül ezt a 6 fordulóból álló játék közben többször befolyásolnia. Jól működő játék a Cuba, mégse nyűgözött le. Ennek én fő okát abban látom, hogy több játék keveréke és egyedül nekem a politikai szál hozott újdonságot, de tulajdonképpen hanem is így de megjelenik ez is más játékokban. Alapvetően egy Puerto Rico hasonmás, de egy kicsit bonyolultabb, illetve szebb. Jó játék a Cuba, érdemes kipróbálni, 3-an is jól működik. 5-en talán elhúzódhat, de szerintem akkor sem vészesen. Nekem nem okozott katarzist, kiemelkedőnek nem éreztem, csak egy jól összerakott játéknak.

RATING: 8.5
This is a really lovely system.  It is, of course, very much a Euro, as it ultimately is only about Victory Point accumulation, and yes it stands on the shoulders of giants.  But, it really doesn't feel like other games.  The system is taught and the number of options available to you all seem engaging.   As you move your machine forward you must destroy what you were initially given, this is all about setting yourself up to create production loops.  There are a number of ways to get yourself in a position to get ahead.  The game is a reasonable length so that as you are burning up your resource board or pushing the market or money you know that there are only six turns so you really are ramping up your game quickly to get as much as you can out of each turn.  

The shipping works well, and the laws are fun.  I like the simple market and the production grid makes for some fun choices.  The art is beautiful and intuitive and it just is a joy to play with.  As soon as you are finished you want to try it again to try out a new strategy.  

I can see my rating going up for this with a couple more plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
First impressions: Was introduced to this game by a person who indicated that I'd like it because it was similar to Puerto Rico. Well, yes and yes; liked it and it does bear certain similarities to Puerto Rico, i.e. plantations, goods, buildings, shipping, a trading house, role selection. But it has something else and it's a quality that I can't quite put my finger on. Yet. I've played it exactly once and was thrilled to have finished in the middle of a five-player pack. Not sure whether it was my capable instructors or the relative simplicity and elegance of the design but I was able to engage almost immediately, whereas with Puerto Rico, I don't think I was fully engaged until I'd had about a dozen games under my belt. Yet, in some ways, Cuba is a trifle more complex. As with PR, the richness of the experience is bound to improve with repeated plays, for a lot of the same reasons - getting to know the buildings and the ways in which each of them can impact play, understanding the roles and how the order in which you play them affects play. Unlike Puerto Rico, all players are in possession of all roles and what matters is the order in which you play them.There appears to be more to learn in this game than Puerto Rico, if you can imagine such a thing. If being in possession of "x" amount of information at any given moment in time is critical to successful PR play, with Cuba it's "x + to-be-determined" amount of information; again, first play. But I liked it A LOT. First game of three on an evening with gaming group and when I got home, its patterns, decisions and thinking processes were with me as I drifted off to sleep.

There have been comparisons to PotE and rightly so, but its a much more advanced engine builder with elements from lots of other games weaved in. There a hints of Puerto Rico with role selection and Caylus with building types. There is also the shipping element from Guatemala Cafe and a personal board for mining resources, harvesting products and laying down buildings. For some reason, that reminded me of the market from Hermagor as you align your worker on an X/Y axis. The most unique feature of the game is the Parliament. Every round new Bills are proposed to affect Taxes, Subsidies, Market etc. Depending on whether or not you win the bid at the end of the round you can affect a total of two of the four laws. Its a really great mechanic and actually brings the theme together in this Euro. I really felt Communism was in effect.

RATING: 6
Rating based on one play. 
I can see what people mean when they call this a cross between Puerto Rico and Caylus. Although it definitely has a different feel from those two games (and other games it's been compared with), it did not provide anything new for me. That doesn't need to be too bad, as I don't consider originality in games very important (I mean, how original is Puerto Rico, anyway?). For me, Cuba's biggest drawback is that it takes pretty long (it's up there with Caylus), but it doesn't make you feel you are actually building towards something. There's not this 'engine' your working on, like in Puerto Rico. Caylus also doesn't have this engine, but at least you can work towards certain goals, like building the prestige buildings (which takes some preperation). All this is lacking in Cuba. You collect resources, spend them for points, and repeat that forcess five more times. Because of the lack of progress, the sixth round feels pretty much the same as the first.
I'd like to play this again to see whether my judgment will change.

RATING: 8
How is this game like Puerto Rico.  Both are islands in the Carribean. Both grow crops on plantations.  Both grow sugar and tobacco.  Both can ship sugar and tobacco for victory points.  Both can build Buildings that let you do cool things.  Both give you victory points at the end of the game for your buildings.  Both have 5 characters you can pick from (the prospector is NOT a character just Cash)both have characters that allow you to ship, work your plantations, let you build buildings, and sell your goods for cash.  But that is all the similarites the two games have.

RATING: 8
This game has lots going on, so if you like that type of game (think Caylus, Puerto Rico, Age of Empires III) then this is for you. The choices you get range from deciding if you are going to try and get goods on a boat; what 4 workers (out of 5) you will select to use and in what order. whether to go for the votes in the parliament bills which influence the game (using the influence from that unused worker mentioned above). Whether to try and build a  building and when you do where to place it on your resource board (as it reduces the amount of resources you can get).

Overall a very good gamers game, with lots of re-playability.

RATING: 7
Fairly interesting game with a lot of paths to victory. Some of the mechanics feel weird (all buildings available at start makes it hard to grasp what to go for) and possibly not completely balanced.

My first game was pretty tight, although I played with 3 others who had played before. I went the anti-shipping route (except for shipping on the first turn to block someone else), using VP generator buildings and veto power to constantly keep the "gain VP depending on your last cards political power" and just never using the Mayor. That combined with getting just enough money and goods to pay for the taxes kept me in the lead the whole time.

Feels like a more complex and better version of Puerto Rico.

RATING: 6
Cuba looks promising, but could have needed a lot more time in development/testing.
It seems to offer many options to the players, but in reality you just choose one strategy in turn 1 and then repeat almost the same actions for 5 more turns that take way too long. You cannot switch strategies or adapt to what your oponents are doing.
Balancing could be better, too. Usually the player who managed to ship the most goods also wins the game because other sources of Victory Points are either very minor or largely unpredictable or both. And who gets to be that player is also largely dependend on chance.

Still a fun game and theme and material are nice, but gameplay seems unfinished.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[u]Key Words:[/u] Worker Placement - Economic - 2-5 Players - 2h

[u]Summarized Opinion:[/u] Tense and difficult economic game that has nothing to do with Puerto Rico.

[u]My Rating Explained:[/u] Very good game. I love to play it.

[u]Verbose Assessment:[/u]

Ok, let me get this off my chest: this is NOT Puerto Rico. I agree that it looks & feels much like it, but game play is totally different.

There's role selection, but you only select for yourself. The interesting thing is that the cards you [i]don't[/i] play are also important for turn order and for voting bills in parliament.

You can construct buildings, but only 3 or 4 each game. Which ones you buy is extremely important, as well as where you build it: each building will eliminate one of the resources or products you can get.

Also, the flow of play is very different: you have to be very careful to not get cut off, and if you get cut off, find a way to cope. It just feels more cut-throat to me.

The politics are interesting, with votes being important but bribes making the difference. Money is hardly necessary, but handy in parliament and often needed to get points by paying your taxes.

Playing Cuba is hard work, but fun and always a nail-biter.

update : Two games tonight.  This game has a tension from start to finish.  Every move is critical to your success.  Strategies rely heavily on the buildings you choose which put you on an all game path to work on.  Very far in advance planning is required.  There are, however, small instances to grab VP's with short term planning.  These come in the form of the laws and shipping.  You definitely have to inch forward point by point because each point you eek out of the system will help you.  The game feels short at 6 rounds but after a couple plays you can get an engine going pretty quickly.

update: I reacquired this and we had some fun doing it again.  In the end it just showed it's lacking two player and was traded out...again.

RATING: 9
I rated this a 9 earlier but some small details have made me change my mind and lower it to an 8.

a) It doesn't seem possible to use a market strategy, i.e. produce stuff that is expensive, sell it and buy cheap stuff to ship, the market fluctuates too fast.

b) I want a more thematic harvest limiting mechanic.

c) The boat manipulation powers are a bit lacking in my opinion, I would rather have had manipulation of single good types on the boats than to swap ship at sea with ship in deck for example...quicker and easier to grasp.

Other than that, a fantastic game :)

RATING: 7
Place market-fresh copies of Caylus, Pillars of the Earth and Puerto Rico into a casserole dish, layer with a bidding/vote mechanic for some meta-game rules, sprinkle in a pinch of salt, mix thoroughly and bake at 300 degrees for 40 minutes. Presto! Serve your new copy of Cuba hot with favorite side dishes. Leave the beer and pretzels in the fridge - you won't be needing 'em. This game is deep and meaty. Tough one to teach because of all the "stuff" going on, but players pick up the mechanics pretty quickly once play begins. May take a couple of games to figure out which buildings work together well. Only a couple of quibbles: the rules-auction mechanic is pretty brutal, with all players who bid paying regardless of whether they win; and the water strategy may be a bit overpowered. I like this one very much despite some very small flaws.

RATING: 6
Liking this so far, although a bit heavier than most games I usually play. The gameplay is pretty simple, but there are a lot of things to think about each turn (especially with the expansion). Having all of the buildings available at all times is just too much to consider.

Update: After a recent play where I grouped the buildings according to building costs, the game was a lot better. It was easier to get a feel for which buildings were available and for what cost. Also, a two-player variant from the forums where you only use two ships helped to improve the game.

Update: Decided to get rid of this after some more plays. Puerto Rico and Santiago de Cuba give a similar feel, but for me those are much better games. Cuba is just too long and has too many rules for the experience it provides.

RATING: 5
Cuba feels like the predecessor to the "hand building" mechanism found in more recent games like Lewis & Clark and Concordia in that you have a hand of action cards that you need to properly sequence each turn. Unlike in those newer games, however, you have the same hand of cards every round. This makes Cuba an incredibly flat experience with minimal growth or variance as the game progresses.

RATING: 7
A fine euro game, in which I really like the way the personal boards work. It is clever and, because of it, the player has to make some hard choices - where to put your tiles is as important as which tiles to take. And I like games that have a spatial aspect.

Everything in [thing=30380][/thing] works well together, and it allows for many different strategies. The only thing against [thing=30380][/thing] is the sheer volume of good euro type games in the market - it is hard to find a place even good games like [thing=30380][/thing], so, it stays out of our attention and the games rotation.

The game is pretty as I come to expect from Michael Menzel.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Good economic "resource allocation" game that plays very well with the maximum number of players (five). Most of the turn actions were used in other, similar, games, but the the way in which you "activate" workers and buildings is quite novel, and requires some serious consideration. If this game appeared on the market some three years earlier, it would be a no-brainer to purchase it. Now, however, you should only consider it if you feel there is a gap between Pillars Of The Earth (less complex, more random) and Agricola (more complex, less random). I like those games a bit better though: POTE is more streamlined and Agricola has a better integrated theme and less downtime. Personally, I therefore have no need to own it.

RATING: 9
Wow.  Cuba is Puerto Rico reimagined.  The trading house is replaced with a power-grid-esque open market, the captain ships require certain types of goods, but you can only load one....it's a very different game with a familiar feel.  There is still role selection, but everyone chooses from 5 roles for 4 turns, and your choice applies to only you.  Activation of buildings and production is different.  It's Puerto Rico, but much different, and possibly better.  Certainly heavier.

Cuba prior to the revolution: Under turbulent circumstances, the villages of the island strive for independent wealth and influence.Who can buy and sell his products and goods on the domestic market profitably or take in the most on the trading ships?
\13\\13\
Who can send the right delegate to parliament in order to influence the government legislative process, or erect distilleries, hotels and banks at the right moment to the benefit of his village? Whoever has accumulated the most victory points at the end of the game, wins. Players earn victory points by shipping merchandise from the harbor, but also by erecting and using buildings, and by abiding by the law.

RATING: 6
For completely different reasons to Santiago de Cuba, I have mixed feeling about Cuba. I really wanted to love this game. It looks nice on the table and the style of it is my cup of tea. After my first experience being taught the game (which I won largely by ignoring shipping of good) there was mostly positive feedback to share about the experience. I enjoy games where you can focus on specialising in a particular strategy or doing a bit of everything. In Cuba there is plenty of choice for which way to take your operation. The simple market mechanic for tracking goods works well (similar wooden token economics to Archipelago and Archon: Glory & Machination) as does card role-based action selection. There is quirky auction bidding system for settling rewards/benefits dished out at the courthouse. My two gripes are one big and one small. It is a modest inconvenience when shedloads of tiles or cards are splayed face-up at the beginning of a game. Annoying this is the case with Cuba. To properly engage with a strategy in the game you need to look at all of the building tiles at the beginning and work out what they are all used for. There is no text on the tiles only icons, so referring to the rules or another player for a translation is necessary. My main problem with this game after an adequate number of  plays is the functionality of the operation board. It has an abstract quality to it, based on a "crossroads" action selection process. I hate it. You need to overbuild locations on the operation board which cancels out the availability of the covered spots. There is decision making required here at a planning level where the future of the game is not yet clearly plotted out. In planning terms, if you make a move then you're bloody well lumped with it for the rest of the game! There is no room for adjustment or redirecting your efforts whatsoever and this detracts from the enjoyment of Cuba.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I love games that have that classic euro feel and this one just feels like an instant classic because of all of the smooth mechanics.  The idea of playing card abilities in a specifically chosen order to counter or beat your opponents to a resource, building, or get a priority on shipping goods is outstanding.  You can tell that many more recent games have used mechanics and design ideas from Cuba.  Any game that has relatively easy to understand gameplay with deep strategic and tactical choices is going to get the best praise from me.  This game really delivers and it'll be fun to continue to explore all of the nuances this game has.  

A lot of people compare this to Puerto Rico, and while I see the comparison, to me it feels much different and is definitely the better game though I know many might disagree with that.  The artwork is stunning.  This has got yo be the best looking game I own or very close to it.  I am still not sure if certain buildings are overpowered, but it will take time to explore all of them and I really look forward to that.  I am eagerly looking forward to this exploration in Cuba and to sometime try the expansion which is supposed to make the base game a lot better (hard to imagine that).

Note:  Sleeved with Mayday Premium Euro Card Size Sleeves and Mayday Mini Euro Premium Sleeves

Note: Plano Box

RATING: 6
Having no expectation prior to playing Cuba, I can't exactly call my experience disappointing.  However, I could safely label it underwhelming.  With the exception of the initial placement of your first building, the rest of the game played out exactly as I had planned from the start.  There was very little tension, no hard choices to make, no need for a plan B.  Each round repeats itself by putting your worker to the field, then activating the buildings so I can turn my product into goods and finally to victory points.  The other two actions oscillate between either grab a free product/resource or buy/sell at the marketplace; and construct a new building or grab the straight VP.  I never shipped, and very rarely did I use the mayor for coins.

The taxes, while interesting, were implemented poorly since they provide everyone the same opportunities to score, and did absolutely nothing to adversely affect the players if they cannot meet the criteria.  The only downside to disregarding the laws is the missed chance in scoring, which can be easily made up if you have a good engine going.

Cuba plays way too long for what little new mechanisms it offers.  Uninspired and repetitive, I quickly turned down a second invitation for another game of this.

RATING: 8.2
This game is often compared with Puerto Rico and Caylus. After opening the box, I can understand Puerto Rico and aftre looking at the building tiles, I can understand  Caylus. But what most comes to mine mind, is Santiago de Cuba, but bigger and more components. I think that’s because we played Santiago de Cuba several times, and it’s called the little brother of Cuba. Cuba being it’s bigger brother means that it’s more full-grown, or it will be more complex. We like Santiago de Cuba very much, so we had big expectations of this one.

When you’ve played Santiago de Cuba a couple of times, getting into Cuba shouldn’t be a problem at all. The basic mechanisms of these game are the same. And probably that’s why, why we were playing this game within 30 minutes after opening the box and punching out the tiles. The game uses a lot of different mechanisms. A free market, elections, shipping, worker placement, resource management. Enough mechanisms to make this a complex game. I don’t know why, but it isn’t complex at all. The different mechanisms make sense. Only the market isn’t used very much. The different laws that comes into play, makes that you’ll have to adjust your strategy during the game. Every victory point that you score will be important, because , at least when playing with 2,  at the end, the score will be close to each-other.

This is not a game that you want to play with your family, but with some more experienced gamers. People who want to think over their next step. It’s not Puerto Rico, neither it’s Caylus, but this is a great game.
